  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e51c8f2e-0a78-4d91-b275-4a802b6f4158'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/692337-why-so-much-hate-for-crichton-s-writing">Why so much hate for Crichton&#39;s writing style? All through the reviews, people are saying the writing&#39;s shitty, or it sucks. I don&#39;t think he&#39;s revolutionizing the craft or anything, but jeez, take it easy.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_692337'><a id="like_count_community_question_692337" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/692337?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">12 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F455373.Sphere">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/692337-why-so-much-hate-for-crichton-s-writing">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/692337-why-so-much-hate-for-crichton-s-writing">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/10738935-dustin-dye"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1447539131i/10738935._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/10738935-dustin-dye">Dustin Dye</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I personally like Crichton's style, and I've read almost every one of his books, including his non-fiction and a number of his works under pseudonyms <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I personally like Crichton's style, and I've read almost every one of his books, including his non-fiction and a number of his works under pseudonyms (although nothing published posthumously). I understand the criticisms though.<br /><br />Strengths:<br /><br />Integrating science into fiction. He naturally provides just enough of the scientific basis to suspend disbelief and move the story forward without bogging down the narrative.<br /><br />Action. His action is (usually) easy to follow, more so that many so-called "action" writers (Ian Fleming, every Star Wars expanded universe novel). The action flows naturally in the story, and is never over-long.<br /><br />Cautionary tales. He took a long-view of technological progress and was cautiously optimistic. He seemed excited about what was possible, but painted worst-case scenarios in his books and movies (Westworld).<br /><br />Weaknesses:<br /><br />His early prose was clumsy (The Andromeda Strain). His later books got increasingly bad (Timeline had an interesting setup, but failed in execution, State of Fear was laughable climate-change-denier propaganda, Next was stupid and appealed to literally no one). Sphere was written during his prime, and is a good example of his style, although Jurassic Park was his masterpiece.<br /><br />Character development. Norman in Sphere was probably his best-developed character in any of his books, but we still only learned enough just to move the plot forward. Sphere was probably the only book where the action was driven by the characters, rather than the other way around. Crichton was good enough as an action writer to compensate for lack of character development. Too many writers can do neither (particularly fantasy/sci-fi writers, whose purple prose is a pathetic attempt to hide this fact).<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">In the beginning of the book, the book kept you interested with the comments of the large cast of characters. later, the story is driven by different <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">In the beginning of the book, the book kept you interested with the comments of the large cast of characters. later, the story is driven by different factors I will not mention do to the spoiling of the book. So yes, it is a page turner.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2319560423" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2319560423?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=crichton-michael">crichton-michael</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2319560423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer76895685918987524">Very much yada yada, humanities vs natural sciences and a wasted plot idea with far too less action and twists.<br/><br/>I still remember how boredom and disappointment grew, after the first few chapters showed the setting and some suspense was built up and then it got lengthy and neither the psychological inner character plot nor the other big mystery plot made much fun or sense, especially because the logic of motivations and thinking capacities and abilities of the protagonists were… average.<br/><br/>Some seem </span>
  <span id="freeText76895685918987524" style="display:none">Very much yada yada, humanities vs natural sciences and a wasted plot idea with far too less action and twists.<br /><br />I still remember how boredom and disappointment grew, after the first few chapters showed the setting and some suspense was built up and then it got lengthy and neither the psychological inner character plot nor the other big mystery plot made much fun or sense, especially because the logic of motivations and thinking capacities and abilities of the protagonists were… average.<br /><br />Some seem to see some kind of deeper philosophical meaning in this one, especially towards the end with something one could call a subtle plot twist, but to me, it was a too constructed, not well designed novel. It tried to be mindfu**ing, but the realization was poor.<br /><br />Possibly, in its time, when there were not much of similar techno- and science-thriller works with Sci-Fi elements for a broader audience, it could have been seen as not so bad because of a lack of alternatives, but especially compared with what today's genre writers produce on a yearly basis and very high level, it sucks. I especially have to laugh about the „psychological thriller“ argument that was used to sell it, because Crichton's characterization and dialogues are so terrible in this one.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/Sphere">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer11427179741232331929">Back in 1993, after reading <i>Jurassic Park</i> (see review of that book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10121047' rel='nofollow noopener'>HERE</a>), I was like in <b>
  <u>Crichton-mode actived!</u>
</b> ;)<br/><br/>So, my next novel had to be by Michael Crichton!<br/><br/>I went to the bookstore and this one was the first book that I checked and after reading the premise, I found it promising. ;)<br/><br/>So, I bought it and started to read it.<br/><br/><b>WoW!!!!!!!</b><br/><br/>Since the beginning I was in shock amalgamated with happiness since the style and mood of the novel was astonishing good. (Of course, by then I hadn't read yet </span>
  <span id="freeText11427179741232331929" style="display:none">Back in 1993, after reading <i>Jurassic Park</i> (see review of that book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10121047" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>), I was like in <b>
  <u>Crichton-mode actived!</u>
</b> ;)<br /><br />So, my next novel had to be by Michael Crichton!<br /><br />I went to the bookstore and this one was the first book that I checked and after reading the premise, I found it promising. ;)<br /><br />So, I bought it and started to read it.<br /><br /><b>WoW!!!!!!!</b><br /><br />Since the beginning I was in shock amalgamated with happiness since the style and mood of the novel was astonishing good. (Of course, by then I hadn't read yet <i>The Andromeda Strain</i>, so the idea of gathering a group of expert scientist to deal with something alien was something new for me :P )<br /><br />I don't know if happens to you, that sometimes if you are reading a novel that it hasn't being made into a film yet (I read the novel like 5 years before to be poorly adapted to a movie) sometimes I imagined some actor or actress to "play" the role of the character in my mind, an amusing thing was by then, in 1993, Sharon Stone was in hype due films like "Basic Instinct" and "Sliver", so when I imagined some actress to fill my image of Dr. Beth Halperin, I thought in Sharon Stone that she eventually got the role!<br /><br />Honest to God! True story!<br /><br />Curiously, 5 years later, Sharon Stone wasn't in hype anymore so, knowing that she was casted in the role that I pictured in my mind was amusing but odd at the same time.<br /><br />I didn't picture Dustin Hoffman for the role of Dr. Goodman, no offense to Mr. Hoffman, which is an actor that I admired a lot, but I thought in Sean Connery for that character.<br /><br />Anyway, the film was a big dissapointment for me since it was very poor adaptation to such excellent book, so if you ever watched the film and you didn't like it, don't worry, that's normal, <b>
  <u>but please</u>
</b>, if that's the reason that you haven't read this novel yet, <b>
  <u>please go and read it!</u>
</b> (Of course, if you have the luck of not having watched the film, please, <b>PLEASE</b>, don't watch it!)<br /><br />I can assure you that the novel is way, way, <b>
  <u>WAY</u>
</b>, better in infinite ways than that mediocre film.<br /><br />I already mentioned the style and mood of the book, I just want to take the topic again to mention that it was a thrilling experience, since you feel that the story is taken very seriously from the scientific angle.<br /><br />All the journey and process of exploring the alien ship is brilliant, you feel that you are there, down in the bottom in the ocean with them, walking with them.<br /><br />There is a major spoiler in this book, that I won't comment here, don't worry, but please avoid at all costs to know that spoiler if you choose to read the book, since it's that kind of spoilers that if you already knew it before of reading the book, simply you will ruin all the fun of actually reading the book and making the investigation along with the scientist group.<br /><br />If you like sci-fi book and/or techno-thriller novels, this is a <b>
  <u>GREAT</u>
</b> option.<br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>

<div id="review_2166701" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2166701" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/142127-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2166701?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/142127-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345353146" href="/book/show/518927.Sphere">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">7th graders and anyone else</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2166701" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10160717666554491721">First, an overlong apologist's review of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton' title='Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'> Michael Crichton</a>. Then, a very short review of Sphere. <br/><br/>(Life update: I am procrastinating).<br/><br/>In my opinion, you can only truly rate a Michael Crichton book by a) the depth and originality of the concept and b) the lucidity of the monologue/essay that will always occur, usually as a rant from some broken visionary genius or another, approx. 4/5 of the way through the book. <br/><br/>Rating Michael Crichton on his prose, either its subtlety or execution, is sort of po</span>
  <span id="freeText10160717666554491721" style="display:none">First, an overlong apologist's review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener"> Michael Crichton</a>. Then, a very short review of Sphere. <br /><br />(Life update: I am procrastinating).<br /><br />In my opinion, you can only truly rate a Michael Crichton book by a) the depth and originality of the concept and b) the lucidity of the monologue/essay that will always occur, usually as a rant from some broken visionary genius or another, approx. 4/5 of the way through the book. <br /><br />Rating Michael Crichton on his prose, either its subtlety or execution, is sort of pointless... there's no doubt that this man is a shitty prose writer, short on synonyms and prone to spend most of his energy on frenetic descriptions of action. He's basically writing scripts... which is why the man gets the Dan Brown money, but no respect from the smarties. <br /><br />So in the man's defense: Michael Crichton is not really a writer, he's an idea man, and that's what attracted me to him in the first place, way back at age eleven. He's the first person I know of since maybe Jules Verne or H.G. Wells to do high quality sci-fi thought experiments, almost always focused on some aspect of humanity's inexorable "progress." In his better books, these are made far more interesting by the aforementioned monologue/essay. This is a hat trick I've never seen another writer (skilled or not) employ so effectively; these speeches almost always provide a (genuinely!) surprising counterpoint, and reframes the "innovations" Crichton describes in terms of different core needs. So suddenly the Timeline is not about BS quantum physics, it is about the rising social need for authenticity. Lost World is not about cashing in on the notion of cloning again; its about successful species as extinction-level events. These speeches are usually so tightly written in comparison to the rest of the text that I've started to think that Michael Crichton starts with them, and build a plot out/afterwards. <br /><br />Sphere is, in my opinion, Michael Crichton's most interesting book on two levels. First, the plot: a truly great parable on the endgame of technology, and the long-sought-after dream of Alladin's magic lamp. I don't want to go too deep, but I'll say that it is, in my humble hypothesis, the uncredited inspiration for the excellent show Lost. While the plot is a highlight, I personally keep this book high on my list of favorites, after all these years, because of the Essay, which breaks with/plays off Crichton's typical form: instead of a broken monologue with a human genius, it takes the form of an italicized, nearly stream of consciousness Socratic dialogue with... something. Again, the writing is problematic (Crichton manages to use the word "foam" 23 times or so in two paragraphs) but, even after all these years, the content still stirs me: the subject is consciousness and the power and meaning of the question mark... again I'll let him do the rest. You can (and should) disagree with his analysis, you can shrug aside his monosyllables, but if something in his notion of What Makes Humanity? doesn't stir you, then you are missing out. You have finally and irrevocably left the seventh grade, that painful age of wondering, in favor of a well-fortified adulthood... and as nice as that sounds, you probably left something essential behind. <br /><br />Hmmm... I may have gotten a little presumptive towards the end there. I'm sure some very smart people have very good reasons for scoffing at Michael Crichton. Allright, let me adjust my position: if you read Sphere, and have thoughts, positive or negative, about the Essay, please write me. If you know of other works, academic or flippant or otherwise, on the same topic, forward 'em. It happens to be one of my favorite things to discuss. <br /><br />Four stars: One demit for bad writing, the fact that I haven't read the book in seven years, the undercooked movie, and my general cowardice (I would seem SO much smarter if I reserved five stars for Gravity's Rainbow...). </span>
  <a data-text-id="10160717666554491721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1011424963" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1011424963" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1011424963?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=made-into-movie">made-into-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=love-the-premise">love-the-premise</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=pulpy-goodness">pulpy-goodness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=survive-this">survive-this</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=love-the-movie">love-the-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=group-in-peril">group-in-peril</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1011424963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15954614476248195683"><br/>I had some idea what to expect when I picked up the late Michael Crichton's sci-fi thriller <i>Sphere</i> because I'd seen <a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0120184/?ref_=nv_sr_1' rel='nofollow noopener'>the movie</a> years ago -- a movie I love by the way despite a lot of lambasting from the critics and grumbling from the book's fans. Sure it isn't perfect (with its moments of cheese and flubs); nevertheless, the exciting, chilling core of Crichton's story is evident and for me the film still stands as a great example of escapist cinema, that mesmerizing addictive blend of science fic</span>
  <span id="freeText15954614476248195683" style="display:none"><br />I had some idea what to expect when I picked up the late Michael Crichton's sci-fi thriller <i>Sphere</i> because I'd seen <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0120184/?ref_=nv_sr_1" rel="nofollow noopener">the movie</a> years ago -- a movie I love by the way despite a lot of lambasting from the critics and grumbling from the book's fans. Sure it isn't perfect (with its moments of cheese and flubs); nevertheless, the exciting, chilling core of Crichton's story is evident and for me the film still stands as a great example of escapist cinema, that mesmerizing addictive blend of science fiction and horror. <br /><br />But I'm probably more forgiving than most. One of my favorite movie genres is space horror. There's something about the claustrophobic squeeze of the 'group in peril' scenario as it hurtles through the freezing, oxygenless void of space where no one can hear you scream. Or the imperiled stranded on an uninhabited, hostile planet where the very environment wants to kill you -- <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0078748/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Alien</a>, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0090605/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Aliens</a>, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt1182345/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Moon</a>, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0062622/?ref_=fn_al_tt_4" rel="nofollow noopener">2001: A Space Odyssey</a>, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0119081/?ref_=fn_al_tt_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Event Horizon</a>, and <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0134847/?ref_=nv_sr_1" rel="nofollow noopener">Pitch Black</a> just to name a few. <br /><br /><i>Sphere</i> is not set in space, but it might as well be. It takes the reader deep into the darkest part of the ocean where unfathomable pressure forces threaten to crush and demolish, where the only breathable oxygen is what you bring with you, where the landscape is as alien and inhospitable as anything found in outer space. <br /><br />A thriller should thrill. It should keep you turning the pages long into the night, white-knuckled and on the edge of your seat. Horror should unsettle and disturb you, compelling you to look over your shoulder and under the bed for that unnamed threat. Science fiction should challenge your concept of reality, bending your mind to what's possible, to <i>what could actually be</i>. In <i>Sphere</i> Crichton is firing on all cylinders as a storyteller, accomplishing all three of these seemingly without any effort at all. <br /><br />It's such a treat to see an author in this much control of his narrative. I read this compulsively, voraciously, rarely coming up for air. I can only imagine the inexorable tension I would have experienced had I not seen the movie and therefore knew most of what to expect. Even so, the whole experience remained thrilling and deliciously unnerving. The pacing is pitch perfect, each devastating reveal coming at the exact right moment. Who or what "Jerry" is becomes a maddening puzzle, his voice and demeanor as terrifying and memorable as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HAL_9000" rel="nofollow noopener">HAL 9000</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15954614476248195683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

The story follows Norman Johnson, a psychologist engaged by the United States Navy, who joins a team of scientists assembled to examine a spacecraft of unknown origin, discovered on the bottom of the Pacific Ocean. The novel begins as a science fiction story, but quickly transforms into a psychological thriller, developing into an exploration of the nature of the human imagination. ..</span>
  <span id="freeText12141536647886761907" style="display:none">Sphere, Michael Crichton<br />Sphere is a 1987 novel by Michael Crichton. It was adapted into the film Sphere in 1998. The novel begins as a science fiction story, but quickly transforms into a psychological thriller, developing into an exploration of the nature of the human imagination. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و سوم ماه اکتبر سال 2004 میلادی<br />عنوان: ک‍ره‌؛ نویسنده: م‍ای‍ک‍ل‌ ک‍رایت‍ون‌؛ م‍ت‍رج‍م‌: ع‍ل‍ی‌ ف‍ام‍ی‍ان‌؛ ت‍ه‍ران‌: ن‍ق‍طه‌‏‫، 1382؛ در 531 ص؛ شابک: 9645548659؛ موضوع: داستانهای علمی تخیلی از نویسندگان ایالات متحده آمریکا - سده 20 م<br /><br />رمان «کره» همچو بیشتر آثار «کرایتون»، پرداختی پر از تنش، و هیجان به همراه دارد. یافتن یک وسیله ی ناشناس، در ژرفای اقیانوس، پای یک روانشناس را، به جریانی دشوار وارد میکند. او که زمانی گزارشی محرمانه برای برخورد با بیگانگان، برای دولت تهیه کرده، پایش به ماجرا کشیده می شود. تلاش برای حل معمای این شیء عجیب، آنها را در جریانی خطرناک و عجیب میکشاند. ...؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_893821156" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/893821156" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/893821156?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/126078.Sphere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=sci-fi-first-contact">sci-fi-first-contact</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer893821156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17594575066764935145">Bullet points seem appropriate when it comes to <i>Sphere</i>, the 1987 science fiction thriller by Michael Crichton. One of the qualities about Crichton's work that I love is the unabashed clarity of his concepts and the dependable navigation of his novels. Crichton doesn't seem like he's lost or should stop to ask for directions. Other writers--those with loftier creative ambitions, perhaps--tend to circle around ideas (like mankind's first contact with extraterrestrials) as if they've never been in </span>
  <span id="freeText17594575066764935145" style="display:none">Bullet points seem appropriate when it comes to <i>Sphere</i>, the 1987 science fiction thriller by Michael Crichton. One of the qualities about Crichton's work that I love is the unabashed clarity of his concepts and the dependable navigation of his novels. Crichton doesn't seem like he's lost or should stop to ask for directions. Other writers--those with loftier creative ambitions, perhaps--tend to circle around ideas (like mankind's first contact with extraterrestrials) as if they've never been in this neighborhood before. Crichton doesn't take the scenic route. He accelerates directly to FINISH, usually provoking quite a literary rush. Usually.<br /><br />* Norman Johnson, a 53-year-old psychologist, is raced across the Pacific Ocean in a Navy helicopter. Skipped from Honolulu to Guam to Pago Pago to wherever he is, Norman is on the FAA's list of crash-site teams and has responded to three airline disasters in the last decade. The Navy has told him that there's been an airplane crash, but information en route has been murky. Out of the blue appear more than a dozen ships: eight Navy destroyers, two Remote Vehicle Support ships, a Mission Support and Supply ship and a couple of Oceanic Survey and Research Vessels, including the <i>John Hawes</i>, which Norman is dropped off on.<br /><br />* Norman is taken to the project commander, Captain Harold Barnes. Informed that there were no survivors, Norman is at a loss as to why he was summoned, his specialty being the treatment of psychological trauma suffered by airline crash survivors. Barnes notifies Norman that there is no aircraft. It's a spacecraft. And estimations are that the crash occurred three hundred years ago. Norman is here because of a report the National Security Council paid him to write for the Carter administration, recommending contingency plans in the event of extraterrestrial contact. Norman's paper, which he considered a joke at the time, was titled "Recommendations for the Human Contact Team to Interact with Unknown Life Forms (ULF)." Norman is taken to meet his recommendations:<br /><br />* Ted Fielding is a 40-year-old astrophysicist at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory. Pompous but cheerful, he's a science communicator who's appeared on television and is a major advocate of SETI, the Search for Extraterrestrial Intelligence. He can't wait to get down to the spacecraft, which is settled "only" a thousand feet beneath the surface. Ted compensates for his perceived lack of accomplishment in his field by hoping this event will be his legacy.<br /><br />* Beth Halpern is a 36-year-old zoologist/ biochemist at the University of Chicago. She's a serious weightlifter and runner. Her recent work has been studying cephalopods. Her thesis is that if not for their three year life span, octopi might be the dominant intelligence on earth. Beth reveals to Norman that Barnes is ex-Navy and as a member of the Defense Science Board, advises the Joint Chiefs of Staff on weapons acquisitions. <br /><br />* Harry Adams is a 30-year-old mathematician at Princeton, the youngest but most vital member of the team. His job is to communicate with the ULF. A prodigy in the field of probability, he has a chip on his shoulder, but is almost always right. He theorizes that the spacecraft is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[not from another planet <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And after the team is briefed by Barnes, Harry confronts the commander for lying to them about divers still searching for the spacecraft door; Harry deduces they've already found it.<br /><br />* Barnes briefs the team that a Navy vessel laying a fiber-optics cable from Honolulu to Sydney hit a snag midway between American Samoa and Fiji. The cable was severed by an obstruction. Sophisticated side-scanning sonar found the obstruction to be a 280 foot tall fin attached to a cylindrical object half a mile long. A segment of the fin cut by a remote vehicle and analyzed strongly suggests that the object could not have originated on this planet. After two weeks of study, geologists confirmed that the coral around the spacecraft is at least three hundred years old, maybe older. If the crew is still alive, the ULF team is to make contact with it.<br /><br />* In the morning, Norman is piloted down to the ocean floor with Ted, who's already working out what they should say to the ULF. They dock at a Navy habitat consisting of five interconnected cylinders where divers have been at work cutting a tunnel to the door. Beth and Harry have been joined by Captain Barnes and the Navy staff who support the contact team: Tina Chan handles electronics. Jane Edmunds is the unit archivist. Alice Fletcher is a chief petty officer and support chief. Rose Levy is the cook. Their genders are not accidental. Barnes explains that Navy studies have shown women to be superior to men for submerged operations.<br /><br />* In a key passage of the novel, the contact team stops before entering the spacecraft to debate each other on the topic of ceremony.<br /><br /><i>Edmunds: "Tape is running."<br /><br />Ted: "I'd like to say a few words."<br /><br />Harry: "Jesus, Ted. Can't you ever let up?"<br /><br />Ted: "I think it's appropriate."<br /><br />Harry: "Go ahead, make your speech."<br /><br />Ted: "Hello. This is Ted Fielding, here at the door of the unknown spacecraft which has been discovered--"<br /><br />Barnes: "Wait a minute, Ted. 'Here at the door of the unknown spacecraft' sounds like 'here at the tomb of the unknown solider.'"<br /><br />Ted: "You don't like it?"<br /><br />Barnes: "Well, I think it has the wrong associations."<br /><br />Ted: "I thought you would like it."<br /><br />Beth:</i> "Cant we just get on with it,<i> please?"<br /><br />Ted: "Never mind."<br /><br />Harry: "What, are you going to pout now?"<br /><br />Ted: "Never mind. We'll do without any commentary on this historic moment."<br /><br />Harry: "Okay, fine. Let's get it open."<br /><br />Ted: "I think everybody knows how I feel. I feel that we should have some brief remarks for posterity."<br /><br />Harry: "Well,</i> make<i> your goddamn remarks!"<br /><br />Ted: "Listen, you son of a bitch. I've had about enough of your superior, know-it-all attitude--"<br /><br />Barnes: "Stop tape please."<br /><br />Edmunds: "Tape is stopped, sir."<br /><br />Barnes: "Let's everyone settle down."<br /><br />Harry: "I consider all this ceremony utterly irrelevant."<br /><br />Ted: "Well, it's not irrelevant. It's appropriate."<br /><br />Barnes: "All right, I'll do it. Roll the tape."<br /><br />Edmunds: "Tape is rolling."<br /><br />Barnes: "This is Captain Barnes. We are now about to open the hatch cover. Present with me on this historic occasion are Ted Fielding, Norman Johnson, Beth Halpern, and Harry Adams."<br /><br />Harry: "Why am I last?"</i><br /><br />* This passage is a snapshot of the novel. The dialogue: not that great. I find it contrived that the team would have a discussion like this while fitted with deep sea diving gear one thousand feet below the surface on the threshold of an alien spacecraft. They should be so terrified that snappy banter is beyond them. However, based on the egos involved, there is a certain honesty to this exchange. In terms of character, <i>Sphere</i> may be the best Michael Crichton has done. The behavior of the team feels consistent with their fields and backgrounds. I thought it was cool to watch scientists spitball their Neil Armstrong speech. <br /><br />That's enough bullet points. Now for what I loved (+) and didn't love (-).<br /><br />+ Crichton takes care assembling a contemporary team of civilian and military professionals to make contact with an alien. How would that contingency plan come together? Who would you choose to talk to an ULF? I found this a damn interesting. I'd want a computer hacker to watch the alien and detect whether it was violating any security protocols, i.e. acting shady. Maybe also a musician or comedian to help lighten the mood. It occurs to me I'm now trying to rewrite Crichton but this is actually a good thing. I was invested in the outcome of his story.<br /><br />+ Lots of intrigue at the front. Crichton introduces an anomaly and compelled me keep reading until his whatsit was explained. What is the alien spacecraft? Who's inside it? Where did it come from? What does it want? All in all, the resolution of these questions were not what I expected, but satisfying. His brainstorm to position the spacecraft on the ocean floor was a very good one. If the situation wasn't terrifying enough, putting the craft in the deep brings another level of unease and dread, as well as opportunity to write about physiology, oceanography and marine biology. <br /><br />+ "Jerry," the ULF, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[communicates with the contact team through decoded type on a computer screen <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and is both fascinating and intimidating.<br /><br />- One of my least favorite characteristics in any book or movie are characters who demonstrate poor job performance. I know that's true to life; I just don't want to read about it. Several of the team members, namely Ted, propose arguments steeped in ignorance that I felt would be, at best, unprofessional for any garden variety scientist, out of character at worst. Ted's knee jerk reaction to anything unexplained is that it has to be alien. He manufactures a conclusion waiting for data. I don't know jack shit about science but even I caught on that this was a fallacy of logic and inherently dangerous to the team.<br /><br />- Crichton's dialogue is written with that amateur writer's template where characters incessantly refer to each other by name. If I had to take a shot of tequila every time someone said, "Norman" I'd have pickled my liver by page 100. Norman even comments at one point that Beth referring to him by his name is getting on his nerves and indicative of the deterioration of her mind, so maybe this was deliberate on Crichton's part. It drove me batty. <br /><br />- The Navy personnel aren't well developed at all. Their <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[deaths <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> carry zero currency. <br /><br />+/- I guess that I should feel disappointed by a story that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[promises alien contact and doesn't have an alien in it, kind of, but not really <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but I think that Crichton navigates the first contact obstacle course extremely well. In my opinion, most stories involving extraterrestrials take a belly flop into ridiculousness once an alien starts walking around and talking. There are a couple of notable examples. I kind of like Crichton's take on this and how he didn't try to explain everything.<br /><br />* <i>Sphere</i> bends human psychology and alien possibility together in ways that thrilled me. It is reminiscent of <i>Jurassic Park</i>, but this came first, so perhaps Crichton was using himself as an influence. The bottom line for me is that it's a fantastic yarn. <i>Sphere</i> was adapted into a big movie in 1998 starring Dustin Hoffman as Norman, Sharon Stone as Beth, Samuel L. Jackson as Harry, Liev Schreiber as Ted and Peter Coyote as Barnes. Watching this cast act is great fun, but when they aren't working off each other, or "Jerry," and stunts have to take over, the suspense flags.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17594575066764935145" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7799640368103379855">2.5 stars. Was debating whether to give it a 2.5 or a 3, with the number changing throughout the book. Man, this was... Lukewarm? Lackluster? Disappointing, definitely. <br/><br/>What I liked <br/>• Interesting premise that held my attention up until the middle of the book, where improbable things start happening and Crichton failed to make them feel real <br/>• Slightly dry, witty style of writing, but again, made the dialogue really unrealistic<br/><br/>What I didn't like<br/>• Terribly written characters.   <span id="freeText7799640368103379855" style="display:none">2.5 stars. Was debating whether to give it a 2.5 or a 3, with the number changing throughout the book. Man, this was... Lukewarm? Lackluster? Disappointing, definitely. <br /><br />What I liked <br />• Interesting premise that held my attention up until the middle of the book, where improbable things start happening and Crichton failed to make them feel real <br />• Slightly dry, witty style of writing, but again, made the dialogue really unrealistic<br /><br />What I didn't like<br />• Terribly written characters. Crichton really overdid it on Beth, a woman scientist who has inferiority and self-esteem issues and talk a lot about how men is constantly being sexist towards her. Crichton has managed to make her into a whiny, insecure, overcompensating person on the issue of her gender. Surely a more complex personality can be used as a plot point/portrayal of imperfect woman/whatever he was trying to achieve?! than a caricature of a feminist which obviously is basically the author's negative impression of them <br />• Pop psychology. The whole Norman trying to analyze his colleagues, but failing, because his observations are written by someone who plainly didn't have knowledge of the field. If there's anything a psychology student hate more than the type of 'psychology' you see on Facebook..... <br />• Unrealistic portrayal of emotions, situations, personalities <br />• The premise isn't unforgettable, regrettably. It's quite underwhelming. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18272144713190050458">Well... that was very <b>strange.</b><br/><br/>This book is a combination of psychological thriller and science fiction. It follows a group of scientists as they investigate what is thought to be an alien vessel that crashed on the ocean floor.<br/><br/>Crichton's musings have a tendency to be deeply speculative of the human condition, but without using language that alienates the audience. I can follow his line of thinking easily without getting lost. <br/><br/>This novel's plot takes a sharp left turn off the path I expected it t</span>
  <span id="freeText18272144713190050458" style="display:none">Well... that was very <b>strange.</b><br /><br />This book is a combination of psychological thriller and science fiction. It follows a group of scientists as they investigate what is thought to be an alien vessel that crashed on the ocean floor.<br /><br />Crichton's musings have a tendency to be deeply speculative of the human condition, but without using language that alienates the audience. I can follow his line of thinking easily without getting lost. <br /><br />This novel's plot takes a sharp left turn off the path I expected it to take, which isn't necessarily a bad thing. It just gave me an odd feeling. I wasn't sure how to anticipate what was coming. <b>Shit got real weird real quick.</b><br /><br />The conclusion of this novel was <b>genuinely shocking.</b> There were moments here and there where I felt authentic fear. I had the urge to keep looking over my shoulder even though the events taking place in the book didn't relate at all to what I was doing in real life. <br /><br />But <i>something</i> was keeping me from investing in the characters. I just couldn't relate to any of them on a personal level, and I didn't much care who survived to the end of the book. <br /><br />It's a well written piece of sci-fi, and definitely a novel that fans of the genre can enjoy. It doesn't compare to Crichton's <i>Jurassic Park,</i> but still a fun ride.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18272144713190050458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13578977006367126139">“You look so unhappy right now.” That is a quote from my boyfriend about my face while reading this. I fell asleep while reading this. In the middle of the day. It also took me almost three months to read. Safe to say, I did not enjoy it. I absolutely love/loved Jurassic Park (who doesn’t) so I was really excited to dive into my second Michael Crichton book. Boy was it a let down. <br/><br/>I’m not going to lie, the writing was really good, the science was fun and the characters were likable but boy was t</span>
  <span id="freeText13578977006367126139" style="display:none">“You look so unhappy right now.” That is a quote from my boyfriend about my face while reading this. I fell asleep while reading this. In the middle of the day. It also took me almost three months to read. Safe to say, I did not enjoy it. I absolutely love/loved Jurassic Park (who doesn’t) so I was really excited to dive into my second Michael Crichton book. Boy was it a let down. <br /><br />I’m not going to lie, the writing was really good, the science was fun and the characters were likable but boy was the plot L A M E. I thought this was going to be some upside down, interdimensional travel, alien attack story but it was far from it. It dragged on forever and when the action finally started it was really unimpressive. REALLY unimpressive. I didn’t feel the same suspense I got from Jurassic Park, but then again it’s Jurassic Park so.. <br /><br />I did like that this book was focused on Psychology and that it explored the shadow self, but I thought I was going to be on the edge of my seat. Which I was not. I was half asleep. That being said, what I really love about Michael Crichton’s books is that he makes science really fun and explains it in a way that someone who isn’t scientifically inclined, AKA me, can understand and really enjoy it. I could also laugh at science-y jokes like I knew what was going one which made me feel smart and cool. <br /><br />So even though this one was a bust I’ll still check it his other books. Jurassic Park awakened my intrigue for Sci Fy and I’m sure he has another spine tingling gem out there.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1749924707" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1749924707" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1749924707?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18739754-sphere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1749924707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6744143169312292774">'Sphere' by Michael Crichton is a superior read in science fiction entertainment! The moments of ROTF are many, primarily because of the intentionally funny dialogue which I adored. (In my checkered history of trying to make a living, I worked as a secretary for a Department Head at a Medical Center/Science facility at a famous University. Science investigators ARE remarkably territorial, argumentative and viciously competitive.) I also was fascinated by the protocols the author mentions that ha</span>
  <span id="freeText6744143169312292774" style="display:none">'Sphere' by Michael Crichton is a superior read in science fiction entertainment! The moments of ROTF are many, primarily because of the intentionally funny dialogue which I adored. (In my checkered history of trying to make a living, I worked as a secretary for a Department Head at a Medical Center/Science facility at a famous University. Science investigators ARE remarkably territorial, argumentative and viciously competitive.) I also was fascinated by the protocols the author mentions that have been created for determining if Earth is dealing with extraterrestrial life if some sort of contact from space seemingly has occurred.<br /><br />The plot of ‘Sphere’:<br /><br />Fifty-three year old Dr. Norman Johnson, psychologist, has been selected as a member of a special American Navy exploration team of civilians. There has been extraterrestrial contact! <br /><br />Well, maybe. Anyway, a peculiar metallic fin has been discovered deep in the ocean buried in a coral bed which is at least 300 years old. The Navy has already conducted tests, the results of which has led them to invite certain scientists to research and perform further tests. Dr. Johnson wrote the paper "Contact with Possible Extraterrestrial Life' for the National Security Council, which is what the USA government is working from in response to the discovery of the fin. Along with developing contingency plans if some sort of space alien ever showed up, Norman provided a list of scientists he felt would work well together in figuring out how to communicate with something which might have completely different cultural reference points than humans from Earth.<br /><br />The civilian team:<br /><br />Theodore 'Ted' Fielding, astrophysicist/planetary geologist and benighted alien enthusiast<br />Elizabeth Halpern, zoologist/biochemist, cautious and smart<br />Harold Adams, mathematician/logician, coldly logical<br />Norman Johnson, psychologist, professional communicator and smoother of group catfights<br /><br />and along for the ride are various lovable technical experts from the Navy who run the huge underwater habitat of connected cylinders built next to the fin over a thousand feet below the ocean's surface.<br /><br />The plan after their submarine trip down to the pressurized habitat is to examine and explore the mysterious fin's exterior for seventy-two hours. No one expected the huge hurricane which drives off the Navy ships supporting the habitat - or sudden appearance of the door in the fin...<br /><br /><br />The dialogue in 'Sphere' reminded me a LOT of the dialogue from another great entertainment:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://youtu.be/T5xcVxkTZzM">https://youtu.be/T5xcVxkTZzM</a><br /><br />'The Thing from Another World', made in 1951, and <i>still</i> my favorite science fiction monster movie.<br /><br />However, the danger which resides within the fin is much more enigmatic and sneakily awful than the alien vegetable in the movie. The book maintains a solid footing in speculative sci-fi from beginning to end, and although the dialogue reminded me of 'The Thing...' movie, the mystery behind the fin reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/95558.Solaris" title="Solaris by Stanisław Lem" rel="nofollow noopener">Solaris</a><br /><br /><br />Wow, is there a lot on the Internet about determining if space aliens are really from space! I decided to stick to the most famous respectable information site, also mentioned in Crichton's 1987 book - SETI:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.seti.org/faq#obs3">http://www.seti.org/faq#obs3</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.seti.org/faq">http://www.seti.org/faq</a><br /><br />And, about the Drake Equation mentioned in 'Sphere':<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drake_equation">https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drake...</a><br /><br />and the Fermi Paradox, or otherwise the "where is everybody? question:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi_paradox">https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi...</a><br /><br />or, the thinking about how 'they' might be found is through tracking down hints the space aliens are looking at us already:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sciencealert.com/scientists-says-they-ve-figured-out-the-best-way-to-find-intelligent-extraterrestrial-life">http://www.sciencealert.com/scientist...</a><br /><br />or, actually, clues that they have<i> already</i> arrived:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://mentalfloss.com/uk/space/27081/5-images-that-suggest-alien-life-on-other-planets">http://mentalfloss.com/uk/space/27081...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6744143169312292774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15723938926742167913">Read this when I was in high school, and was one of the few times I've been up nearly all night reading, saying to myself every hour, "I need to go to sleep," but then couldn't put it down. The story has a very tense, claustrophobic feel, and rightly so, as the characters are trapped at the bottom of the o</span>
  <span id="freeText15723938926742167913" style="display:none">Read this when I was in high school, and was one of the few times I've been up nearly all night reading, saying to myself every hour, "I need to go to sleep," but then couldn't put it down. The movie adaptation was atrocious, so don't judge this story based on the film (I always pictured Ted Knight in the role of Ted, not Liev Schreiber [though Mr Schreiber is a talented actor]). The story has a very tense, claustrophobic feel, and rightly so, as the characters are trapped at the bottom of the ocean.<br /><br />One of my all-time favorite quotes about learning vs. experience: "Do you want to understand how to ride a bicycle, or do you want to get on and start riding?"<br /><br />A very fun read.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13555222-alex-deranged-kittycat?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1779942043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7145817085808168593">Well, I had no idea there was a Sphere book. I only saw the movie and thought that was it. Not to mention that the book is written by the same author as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6424171.Jurassic_Park' title='Jurassic Park by Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jurassic Park</a>.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>A group of American scientists are rushed to a huge vessel that has been discovered resting on the ocean floor in the middle of the South Pacific. What they find defines their imaginations and mocks their attempts at logical explanation. It is a spaceship of phenomenal dimensions, apparently, undamaged by its fall from the sky. </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText7145817085808168593" style="display:none">Well, I had no idea there was a Sphere book. I only saw the movie and thought that was it. Not to mention that the book is written by the same author as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6424171.Jurassic_Park" title="Jurassic Park by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Jurassic Park</a>.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>A group of American scientists are rushed to a huge vessel that has been discovered resting on the ocean floor in the middle of the South Pacific. What they find defines their imaginations and mocks their attempts at logical explanation. It is a spaceship of phenomenal dimensions, apparently, undamaged by its fall from the sky. And, most startling, it appears to be at least three hundred years old...</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Both the book and the movie have the same ending. But the journey to get to that ending is quite different at times. So if you only watched the film, or you only read the book, I think you should give the other version a try. I liked them both.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1478416072ra/21069575.gif" width="490" height="202" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The nature of the sphere itself remains a mystery, and I'm pleased and disappointed with that at the same time. I'm pleased because not everything has to explained, and disappointed because I really wanted to know whether it was an alien being or just a machine.</span>

<div id="review_2328170842" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2328170842" />
    <a title="Clare" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65154201-clare"><img alt="Clare" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540789471p2/65154201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2328170842?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Clare" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Clare" href="/user/show/65154201-clare">Clare</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061990557" href="/book/show/9023471-sphere">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2328170842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7933561412449918697"><b>OVERALL AVERAGE RATING: 3.9</b><br/><br/><b>Characters: 4/5</b><br/>Although it was sizable cast, each character had such a distinctive and memorable personality. The book to some extent was about how these different personalities would react in a crisis situation, and overall, I think Crichton did a good job fleshing that out. <br/><br/><b>Plot: 4/5</b><br/>The plot was well-paced, and had enough mystery and intrigue to keep it compelling. It certainly had some twists and turns that I wasn’t expecting, and it was fun to see that unfold. <br/><br/><b>Writ</b></span>
  <span id="freeText7933561412449918697" style="display:none"><b>OVERALL AVERAGE RATING: 3.9</b><br /><br /><b>Characters: 4/5</b><br />Although it was sizable cast, each character had such a distinctive and memorable personality. The book to some extent was about how these different personalities would react in a crisis situation, and overall, I think Crichton did a good job fleshing that out. <br /><br /><b>Plot: 4/5</b><br />The plot was well-paced, and had enough mystery and intrigue to keep it compelling. It certainly had some twists and turns that I wasn’t expecting, and it was fun to see that unfold. <br /><br /><b>Writing style: 3.5/5</b><br />As interesting as the characters and plot were, there wasn’t anything particularly remarkable about the writing itself. I don’t know how much of it is due to Crichton’s style or to the nature of the sci-fi genre, but the writing was simple and straightforward. None of that flowery, descriptive language that I’m used to reading in fantasy books. On the plus side, it did make for an easier and faster read.<br /><br /><b>Entertainment: 4/5</b><br />Admittedly, I was a little skeptical at first when a friend insisted I read this book. I don’t usually do sci-fi, let alone a mysterious alien spaceship in the middle of the ocean. But for my first Michael Crichton novel, I was pretty satisfied overall. It was different from what I usually read but certainly a welcome change of pace.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7933561412449918697" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6216248525015665059" style="display:none">I'm reading Nick Cutter's upcoming horror novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22119322.The_Deep" title="The Deep by Nick Cutter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Deep</a> right now and it's reminded me of how much I enjoyed <i>Sphere</i>, Michael Crichton's best novel. It was my favorite book when I was a kid. I remember what a page-turner it was and how much the tension kept ratcheting up until it was unbearable. I remember the plot being extremely clever as well, and how much of it was dependent on less of the sci-fi aspects and more on the characters themselves. <span id="freeTextContainer12367659180532329180">Five civilian experts were selected to join a small Navy crew to the south Pacific. They were to explore a spacecraft at a thousand feet depth that had crashed over three hundred years ago.<br/><br/>This was such a fun sci-fi thriller. It gives you claustrophobia induced experience of being in an underwater habitat - a damp, confined space. It also has the psycho factor, Normaaaaan. It gives me the creeps. The ending, though, was not what I had expected.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3280023319" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3280023319" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3280023319?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18739754-sphere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=standalone">standalone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3280023319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8226083098129158245">This was an exciting and very fast-paced read, and for the most part I enjoyed it. It was my first time reading anything by Crichton, although I had watched the <i>Jurassic Park</i> movies several years back.<br/><br/>The story opens with our main character, a psychologist named Norman, being flown to a remote location in the Pacific Ocean where he expects to encounter a plane crash with survivors that need counseling. Instead, he finds that he’s going to be part of a team of scientists who will be going more th</span>
  <span id="freeText8226083098129158245" style="display:none">This was an exciting and very fast-paced read, and for the most part I enjoyed it. It was my first time reading anything by Crichton, although I had watched the <i>Jurassic Park</i> movies several years back.<br /><br />The story opens with our main character, a psychologist named Norman, being flown to a remote location in the Pacific Ocean where he expects to encounter a plane crash with survivors that need counseling. Instead, he finds that he’s going to be part of a team of scientists who will be going more than 1000 feet down to the ocean floor to investigate a large, mysterious object that is believed to be an alien spaceship.<br /><br />The story unfolds in a very movie-like way, or at least it kept reminding me of the horror movies I enjoyed in my youth, around the same time this book was published in 1987. Not so much because it was scary, although there were some creepy and tension-filled moments, but because of the story-telling techniques. The main characters all have very distinct and rather cookie-cutter personalities, and they naturally clash with each other in their cramped and isolated environment. There are various twists as the plot unfolds, some of which I found obvious and some of which I didn’t. There’s a sense of tension almost from the beginning that slowly mounts and mounts, and naturally <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[people start dying left and right. Then we end with the typical “is it really over?” style ending in which it’s blatantly hinted that Beth didn’t actually forget about the Sphere. We really can’t be 100% sure about Harry either. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />As I mentioned, I don’t think this is really a scary story, but there was definitely tension for me as I read it. Part of it was the underwater environment which for some reason tends to creep me out, more so than stories set in space where the atmosphere-related dangers are similar. There was also often a “noooo, don’t do that!” feeling when characters did things that weren’t always necessarily stupid, although sometimes they were, but that I was sure would lead to a bad end.<br /><br />The story had some gender and racial stereotypes that made me uncomfortable at times, in part because I wasn’t sure what the author was trying to say, but I decided to give him the benefit of the doubt that he was trying to raise awareness of how the stereotypes and prejudice that people can be inundated with may harm (generic comments, but might be kind of spoilerish if one were planning to read the book soon) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[their mental health and their self-perception <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />So it wasn’t without its issues. There were some eye-rolling moments, and I wasn’t too crazy about the end, but I think mostly I just enjoyed being so absorbed by the book while I was reading it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8226083098129158245" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15017590144895043779" style="display:none">Michael Crichton's books can be hit or miss affairs,this one was definitely a miss.<br /><br />An alien vessel crashes on the ocean floor.It is a three hundred year old spaceship.               <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=sff-audio">sff-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1553497649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4911883419432089598"><b>Executive Summary:</b> A strong start and a pretty strong finish, but I found a lot of the last quarter or so on the slow side. This is a pretty solid 3.5 stars that could be rounded up or down depending on my mood at the time.<br/><br/><b>Audiobook:</b> Apparently this book had been released in audio before, but for some reason Brilliance Audio seems to be (re)releasing a bunch of his books recently. Scott Brick does his usual quality job. Whenever you see Mr. Brick's name on an audiobook, you know you're going to </span>
  <span id="freeText4911883419432089598" style="display:none"><b>Executive Summary:</b> A strong start and a pretty strong finish, but I found a lot of the last quarter or so on the slow side. This is a pretty solid 3.5 stars that could be rounded up or down depending on my mood at the time.<br /><br /><b>Audiobook:</b> Apparently this book had been released in audio before, but for some reason Brilliance Audio seems to be (re)releasing a bunch of his books recently. Scott Brick does his usual quality job. Whenever you see Mr. Brick's name on an audiobook, you know you're going to get a good reading. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Full Review</u>
</b><br />I came into this book thinking it was a reread. I did a handful of books by Mr. Crichton when I was in high school, and I thought this was among them. As I got further into the book, I became convinced otherwise.<br /><br />I found the beginning very interesting. A psychologist is brought in to help with a crash that turns out to be a spaceship on the bottom of the ocean. I liked the mystery and investigation aspect of the story, more than the viewpoint of the main character itself though.<br /><br />As the plot develops and we learn more about not only the ship, but the sphere it contains, I found my mind starting to wander. I didn't get attached to any of the characters. I found myself annoyed by most of the scientists. Several of them seemed to be more concerned about being published and/or their place in history than the actual investigation itself. I've always been more of an engineer than a scientist, but I don't know why anyone would want to deal with that.<br /><br />As with the other Michael Crichton books I've read, this one takes science and posits some plausible seeming possibilities. He always seemed to have a knack for the techno-thriller in a way that doesn't feel cheesy and over the top.<br /><br />I'm not sure if I was disappointed with the truth of the Sphere, or if my detatchment from the characters just got to me, but by about the 50% mark, I found my mind starting to wander a bit. The ending was pretty strong though, and probably saved it from me rounding down to a three.<br /><br />I've been wanting to take a break from SFF this year, and while this is definitely still in the Sci-Fi wheelhouse, it's more of a thriller with a sci-fi premise than a pure science fiction book. I didn't enjoy it nearly as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7669.Timeline" title="Timeline by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Timeline</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6424171.Jurassic_Park" title="Jurassic Park by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Jurassic Park</a>, but I'm glad I finally read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4911883419432089598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer603581526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2611345486758449537">The first half of 'Sphere' is probably the greatest thing Crichton ever wrote. The amount of plot twists, each superbly executed, is excellent. However, the second half of the book falls apart as it becomes a different type of story than the first half. Still a very good read and one of Crichton's top five best books, but I just found myself wishing the story could have unfolded at the same high level from the first page to the last.</span>
