<div id="review_2442214638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2442214638" />
    <a title="Toe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1721150-toe"><img alt="Toe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525037665p2/1721150.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2442214638?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toe" href="/user/show/1721150-toe">Toe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/126078.Sphere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721150-toe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2442214638&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2442214638" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8675571357429932398">Objective Summary<br/><br/>Norman Johnson is a psychologist from the University of California, San Diego. The U.S. Navy summons him to consult on a plane crash in the Pacific Ocean. When he arrives at the site, he learns it is not a recent commercial or military plane crash, but an alien spaceship on the bottom of the Pacific Ocean. Based on the coral growth calculations of marine biologists and oceanographers, the spaceship arrived about 300 years ago. <br/><br/>The Navy assembles an Unknown Life Form welcome squa</span>
  <span id="freeText8675571357429932398" style="display:none">Objective Summary<br /><br />Norman Johnson is a psychologist from the University of California, San Diego. The U.S. Navy summons him to consult on a plane crash in the Pacific Ocean. When he arrives at the site, he learns it is not a recent commercial or military plane crash, but an alien spaceship on the bottom of the Pacific Ocean. Based on the coral growth calculations of marine biologists and oceanographers, the spaceship arrived about 300 years ago. <br /><br />The Navy assembles an Unknown Life Form welcome squad based on recommendations Norman drafted for a top secret government project six years earlier. Norman is joined by mathematician Harry Adams, zoologist Beth Halpern, astrophysicist Ted Fielding, Navy project commander Hal Barnes, and four other Navy support personnel. The Navy had already constructed a deep water habitat near the spaceship, and the ULF team descends to the ocean floor. The team investigates the spacecraft and discovers it was actually manufactured by the United States in the year 2043. They deduce the craft entered a black hole and traveled back in time to arrive at the bottom of the Pacific Ocean hundreds of years before it was manufactured.<br /><br />In addition to advanced materials technology and optoelectronics, the craft houses a silver sphere that is 30 feet in diameter, perfectly round, and stunningly beautiful. Harry Adams enters the sphere then reemerges without remembering going in. The team receives contact from a seemingly alien life form that communicates in numeric code through their computer screens. Harry cracks the code, and they can then converse in English. The life form identifies itself as Jerry. Norman notices from Jerry’s statements that it seems like an emotional child. Jerry soon turns tyrannical by manifesting dangerous creatures. A giant squid attacks the habitat several times. Swarms of jellyfish, shrimp, and eggs appear without explanation. The team experiences a series of disasters and mishaps. The cylinders that comprise the underwater habitat are destroyed and flooded one by one.<br /><br />Over time, only Norman, Harry, and Beth remain, and all the others have died. The three survivors all entered the sphere at different times and learned that it granted them the “power to make things happen just by thinking of them. The power to make [their] thoughts real.” Whatever fears they dwelled on, consciously or subconsciously, became reality. Harry feared squid, so the giant squid attacked. Norman feared jellyfish, so they arrived. Beth feared formless, unknown power, so an underwater tornado nearly killed her. Jerry was not an alien life form, but simply Harry’s subconscious. Evidently the sphere could also manifest positive realities, like when Harry wanted shrimp for dinner and a school appeared, but we do not see much of that as the team becomes increasingly stressed and frightened. <br /><br />In the end, Norman has the chance to save himself on the escape submarine and leave Beth and Harry to die in the deep water habitat. But he turns around and saves them just in time. When they are back safely on the surface, they agree that humanity is not advanced enough to wield the power offered by the sphere. This group of highly educated, highly intelligent individuals almost killed themselves in a matter of days. To save humanity from its own destruction, Norman, Beth, and Harry agree to forget the sphere. Since they all have the power, it comes true. The sphere disappears, and the Navy tapes that were recording the whole time change to a routine deep water habitat failure. <br /><br /><br />Subjective Thoughts<br /><br />This was an intelligent page-turner. The premise and execution were great, and I enjoyed the scientific tidbits and fun facts. While the ending was coherent, it felt underwhelming. And it left me with questions. Was there really no way to focus on the positive aspects of the imagination? Not one of the survivors could have consciously used the power to manifest some great technology before they let it slip away forever? They couldn’t solve world hunger, invent endless clean energy, cure disease, or eliminate car alarms before they forgot the sphere? All of those would have made the world better for humanity, and it’s unclear to me why they couldn’t have taken a swing at any one or all of those. Wasn’t their fear exacerbated by the environment they were in? And wouldn’t that fear have subsided once they returned to the comfort of their homes? Crichton seemed to think not, and perhaps I missed the whole point. But it felt like they gave short shrift to the whole notion of the positive possibilities of the sphere. It seems so ungrateful and so wasteful. Maybe I’m deluding myself into thinking I could have done better, and maybe if given the power I’d just unleash the clown from “It” onto the world. Or maybe I would rid the world of car alarms and glitter, the two worst inventions of all time.<br /><br /><br />Memorable Quotes<br /><br />“Norman had recommended a ULF [Unknown Life Form] team of four—an astrophysicist, a zoologist, a mathematician, a linguist—and a fifth member, a psychologist, whose job would be to monitor the behavior and attitude of the working team members.”<br /><br />“[The Drake equation is] a probability equation. P=fpnhf1fifc What it means is that the probability, p, that intelligent life will evolve in any star system is a function of the probability that the star will have planets, the number of habitable planets, the probability that simple life will evolve on a habitable planet, the probability that intelligent life will evolve from simple life, and the probability that intelligent life will attempt interstellar communication within five billion years. That’s all the equation says. But the point is that we have no facts. We must guess at every single one of these probabilities. And it’s quite easy to guess one way, as Ted does, and conclude there are probably thousands of intelligent civilizations. It’s equally easy to guess, as I do, that there is probably only one civilization. Ours.”<br /><br />“There are really only two kinds of child prodigies—mathematical and musical. Some psychologists argued there was only one kind, since music was so closely related to mathematics. While there were precocious children with other talents, such as writing, painting, and athletics, the only areas in which a child might truly perform at the level of an adult were in mathematics or music. Psychologically, such children were complex: often loners, isolated from their peers and even from their families by their gifts, for which they were both admired and resented. Socialization skills were often retarded, making group interactions uncomfortable.”<br /><br />“[T]he vast Pacific Ocean, which amounted to half the total surface area of the Earth, had an average depth of two miles.”<br /><br />“Every conception of extraterrestrial life, whether by a movie maker or a university professor, has been basically human—assuming human values, human understanding, human ways of approaching a humanly understanding universe. And generally a human appearance—two eyes, a nose, a mouth, and so on. . . . [T]hat’s obviously nonsense. . . . And when you come to a new life form, the differences may be literally incomprehensible. The values and ethics of this new life form may be utterly different.”<br /><br />“The alien technology might be purely mental, with no visible hardware at all. This issue was at the heart of congruity theory, which said that unless the aliens were remarkably similar to us, no exchange of information was likely.”<br /><br />“Every living creature must maintain the integrity of its physical borders. That’s defense, and we can’t have life without it.”<br /><br />“Psychology’s not a science, it’s a form of superstition or religion. It simply doesn’t have any good theories, or any hard data to speak of. It’s all soft. All this emphasis on emotions—you can say anything about emotions, and nobody can prove you wrong.”<br /><br />“In general, people who aren’t in touch with their emotions tend to think their emotions are unimportant.”<br /><br />“But the bear differs from you in one important way. It cannot perform the activity you call imagining. It cannot make mental images of how reality might be. It cannot envision what you call the past and what you call the future. This special ability of imagination is what has made your species as great as it is. Nothing else. It is not your ape-nature, not your tool-using nature, not language or your violence or your caring for young or your social groupings. It is none of these things, which are all found in other animals. Your greatness lies in imagination. The ability to imagine is the largest part of what you call intelligence. You think the ability to imagine is merely a useful step on the way to solving a problem or making something happen. But imagining it is what makes it happen.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="8675571357429932398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2186421349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2186421349" />
    <a title="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62203305-sumi-s-books"><img alt="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580352861p2/62203305.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2186421349?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" class="user" itemprop="url" name="✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨" href="/user/show/62203305-sumi-s-books">✨Sumi&#39;s Books✨</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2186421349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13273204115843815712">A great book!<br/>Dr. Norman Johnson is flown to the middle of the South Pacific and dredged thousands of feet below the surface to investigate a so-called plane crash. The operation is military-run and Norman suspects from the very beginning that there is more than meets the eye. Upon meeting his other team members and being briefed by Captain Barnes, who is head of the operation, he soon learns that they're actually investigating an alien spacecraft more than 300 years old at the bottom of the ocea</span>
  <span id="freeText13273204115843815712" style="display:none">A great book!<br />Dr. Norman Johnson is flown to the middle of the South Pacific and dredged thousands of feet below the surface to investigate a so-called plane crash. The operation is military-run and Norman suspects from the very beginning that there is more than meets the eye. Upon meeting his other team members and being briefed by Captain Barnes, who is head of the operation, he soon learns that they're actually investigating an alien spacecraft more than 300 years old at the bottom of the ocean. But where has this spacecraft been? Why was it built? And more importantly what did it bring back with it?<br />This book was an amazing read and keeps you riveted from beginning to end. Crichton has a simplicity to his writing that is both descriptive and easy to follow. The story is very well thought out. And though at times it does have the feel of an adventure on the high seas it does borderline on the horror genre.<br />This was my first book from Michael Crichton. I have seen nearly all his films but this was the very first book that I picked up from him. Let me tell you I was not disappointed. This book was both fascinating and very well researched. Crichton does his job well at mastering the art of the written word and keeping the reader in the jaws of suspense.<br />Though it does differ from the film, sometimes minutely and other times quite greatly, both have the same feel and even the same terror to them. I'd have to say in this case I like the book and the film equally which is a rarity. You can see in reading the book why they had to leave some parts out in the film and why they decided to go in different directions with portions of the plot. It makes it much more mysterious and way more creepy.<br />I would definitely recommend this book to anyone with a taste for adventure mixed with a little bit of thrills and horror.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13273204115843815712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer530033286" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6837460123285292719">It's been such a long time since I've read a book by Michael Crichton, but after reading Sphere, I was completely blown away, it's just become one of my all-time favorites by him, right up there with Jurassic Park and The Andromeda Strain! I jumped into it expecting just a typical and predictable Sci-Fi Underwater adventure, nope, nothing of the sorts! Yes it is Science Fiction, but also a mixture of a psychological thriller, developing into an exploration of the nature of human imagination.<br/><br/>A gr</span>
  <span id="freeText6837460123285292719" style="display:none">It's been such a long time since I've read a book by Michael Crichton, but after reading Sphere, I was completely blown away, it's just become one of my all-time favorites by him, right up there with Jurassic Park and The Andromeda Strain! I jumped into it expecting just a typical and predictable Sci-Fi Underwater adventure, nope, nothing of the sorts! Yes it is Science Fiction, but also a mixture of a psychological thriller, developing into an exploration of the nature of human imagination.<br /><br />A group of Scientists discover a shipwreck in the middle of the South Pacific, thousands of feet beneath the sea, but it is no typical shipwreck, (nothing you'd expect from a Clive Cussler novel), it is a spaceship from another world, apparently undamaged, and no lifeforms aboard, or are there?<br /><br />The group led by Psychologist Norman Johnson, Mathematician Harry Adams, and Zoologist Beth Halpern, set up their own base of operations beneath the surface, then search the mysterious craft, inside and outside, and discover something inside unimaginable in all of mankind, a sphere. But what the group does not know, is it manmade or of extraterrestrial origin. But what they will soon discover, is what it can do and what it is capable of. Soon all 3 members are put to the test, facing the wonders and dangers of this mysterious object.<br /><br />A winner by the late Michael Crichton, well thought out, not too over-detailed, very easy to follow, beautifully written! Makes you wonder about other life in the universe. Recommended to lovers of Science Fiction!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6837460123285292719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1928434083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1928434083" />
    <a title="Margaret Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4024068-margaret-taylor"><img alt="Margaret Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325690808p2/4024068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1928434083?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret Taylor" href="/user/show/4024068-margaret-taylor">Margaret Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1928434083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15872679477346702885">A black man, a white man, and a white woman all get the power to warp reality to their will. The black man and the white woman can't handle the power and nearly get the group killed. The white man saves the day by being more emotionally stable than them.<br/><br/>ಠ_ಠ<br/><br/>I don't think Michael Crichton meant <i>Sphere</i> to come out sounding that way, but his characters are so paper-thin there's nothing left of them but their stereotypes.<br/><br/>Norman Johnson, professor of psychology, gets called in by the Navy to investiga</span>
  <span id="freeText15872679477346702885" style="display:none">A black man, a white man, and a white woman all get the power to warp reality to their will. The black man and the white woman can't handle the power and nearly get the group killed. The white man saves the day by being more emotionally stable than them.<br /><br />ಠ_ಠ<br /><br />I don't think Michael Crichton meant <i>Sphere</i> to come out sounding that way, but his characters are so paper-thin there's nothing left of them but their stereotypes.<br /><br />Norman Johnson, professor of psychology, gets called in by the Navy to investigate a plane crash in the Pacific Ocean. The downed plane turns out to be a spacecraft. Because of Norman's previous work on group dynamics, the Navy has given him the job of keeping the crew from freaking out in the face of alien life. Norman and a team of scientists he hand-selected go to a deep-sea habitat to investigate the wreck.<br /><br />Some of what happens next is spoileriffic. Suffice to say that whoever comes into contact with a certain sphere gets reality-warper powers. It involves time travel.<br /><br />For the first few chapters, I found Crichton's prose refreshingly sparse. After a while it gets wooden. Check this out:<br /><br /><blockquote>Norman suddenly felt overwhelmed. He sat on his bunk, holding the notebook in his hands. Finally he looked at a couple of pages, filled with Ted's large, enthusiastic scrawl. A photograph fell onto his lap. He turned it over. It was a photo of a red Corvette. And the feelings just overwhelmed him. Norman didn't know if he was crying for Ted, or crying for himself, because it was clear to him that one by one, they were all dying down here. He was very sad, and very afraid.</blockquote><br /><br />Crichton doesn't explore the implications of the magic sphere in nearly as much depth as he could have. Why aren't characters inundated with pink elephants when they desperately try not to think about them? Why can't characters imagine their way out of problems? The only thing the power seems to do is throw monsters at the habitat.<br /><br />The plot runs on the characters' terrible decisions. When the Navy learn that a typhoon is bearing down on the site of the wreck, which isn't going anywhere, they hasten to send the characters to the underwater habitat just in time to trap them down there. When jellyfish of an unknown species swarm the habitat, one of the Navy officers decides it's time to go for a swim. Somebody rigged the habitat with a deadman's switch that takes away their only means of escape unless they press a button. On the outside of the habitat.<br /><br />That's not even what bugs me the most about this book – it's the three main characters, Harry Adams, Beth Halperin, and Norman.<br /><br />Harry isn't that bad. He's actually competent, and when his powers get the best of him, it's because of normal human fears. And it's gratifying to see him survive.<br /><br />I like women villains. I know real women can be any one of sex-obsessed, power hungry, manipulative, hysterical (Crichton literally uses this word), incompetent, and bitter. But why is Beth all of these things? And she never misses a chance to mention she's a woman?<br /><br />Crichton's description of her is disturbing:<br /><br /><blockquote>Beth, with her lack of self-esteem, her deep core of self-hate, had gone inside the sphere, and now she was acting with the power of the sphere, but without stability to her thoughts. Beth saw herself as a victim who struggled against her fate, always unsuccessfully. Beth was victimized by men, victimized by the establishment, victimized by research, victimized by reality. In every case she failed to see how she had done it to herself.</blockquote><br /><br />I don't know what message Crichton is trying to convey here, but I think he had a bone to pick with somebody.<br /><br />Norman's a schlub. The problems with the mission are largely his fault – it was his job to pick a team of scientists who would be unlikely to crack under stress. He picks a team of scientists with low stress tolerance who all hate each other. When consequences follow, he doesn't feel remorse. He just rescues Harry and the hysterical Beth because he can handle the sphere-power better than them.<br /><br />I think the problem here is that Crichton was trying to write a book about emotions … and he sucks at it. It's telling that he equated emotions with space aliens in this book. He tried to get into his characters' heads and instead knocked things over. I was rooting for the giant squid.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15872679477346702885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jayanth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6388515-jayanth"><img alt="Jayanth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514739444p2/6388515.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1941339135?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jayanth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jayanth" href="/user/show/6388515-jayanth">Jayanth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6388515-jayanth?shelf=used-books">used-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6388515-jayanth?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1941339135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9663056686201576807">Update: Lowered the rating to 3 from 4 stars. Was just thinking of Crichton's works I've read and this was so underwhelming in terms of what it promised and what it delivered by the end. <br/><br/>I had a claustrophobic feeling all through while reading this thrilling book about the discovery and the consequent scientific expedition to understand the alien spacecraft lying on the pacific ocean bed, a 1000-feet deep. <br/><br/>A team is assembled and is sent out into the middle of nowhere in the Pacific Ocean. The s</span>
  <span id="freeText9663056686201576807" style="display:none">Update: Lowered the rating to 3 from 4 stars. Was just thinking of Crichton's works I've read and this was so underwhelming in terms of what it promised and what it delivered by the end. <br /><br />I had a claustrophobic feeling all through while reading this thrilling book about the discovery and the consequent scientific expedition to understand the alien spacecraft lying on the pacific ocean bed, a 1000-feet deep. <br /><br />A team is assembled and is sent out into the middle of nowhere in the Pacific Ocean. The scientific research team consists of an astrophysicist, a biologist, a mathematician and a psychologist along with a few others. Most of the narration follows the psychologist as he is therethere to observe the whole team as they are locked inside an artificial habitat on the ocean floor for a few days and it can be a pretty tumultuous experience for them considering the claustrophobic setting and the uncertainty of their discovery. <br /><br />The mystery of the alien spacecraft is well built-up as the team tries to make sense of what they are looking at. Although I did not like the way the story changed or 'the explanation' of the spacecraft and what's inside, I felt Crichton did a good job at creating a unique situation that results from his choices for the story. <br /><br />I liked the metaphysical commentary on what it might mean to mankind when, IF, we make contact with extraterrestrial life. <br /><br /><blockquote>For example, astronomers believed that if we made contact with extraterrestrials, mankind would be so shocked that wars on Earth would cease, and a new era of peaceful cooperation between nations would begin. But historians thought that was nonsense. They pointed out that when Europeans discovered the New World—a similarly world-shattering discovery—the Europeans did not stop their incessant fighting. On the contrary: they fought even harder. Europeans simply made the New World an extension of pre-existing animosities. </blockquote><br /><br />What I did not like was the absurdity of some of the choices the crew makes. This is so frustrating to say the least. I see movies like Alien and the sequels getting cult fan following, but how can a specialist team not have the sense to do stupid stuff that common sense should ideally prevent them from doing. This is a big cliched trope in both books and movies, the creators do it so that these stupid choices result in close shaves and thrilling sequences or whatever. I'm ranting, the characters in this book were not as bad is the recent Alien movies, but I was triggered nevertheless.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9663056686201576807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Andrea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1881113-andrea"><img alt="Andrea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537392299p2/1881113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea" href="/user/show/1881113-andrea">Andrea</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1356373943" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6301534854698303402">Michael Crichton had some amazing ideas, as pretty much all the blurbs on his books catch my interest. But, and that's a big "but", the writing is terrible. Sphere is heavy on dialogue, flat characters, and sensational action scenes. It's obvious that Crichton's true calling was screenplay writing, and so far all of the books that I've read by him were made into movies. He often wrote about interesting and very much possible technologies, but I so wish his execution was better. Nevertheless, thi</span>
  <span id="freeText6301534854698303402" style="display:none">Michael Crichton had some amazing ideas, as pretty much all the blurbs on his books catch my interest. But, and that's a big "but", the writing is terrible. Sphere is heavy on dialogue, flat characters, and sensational action scenes. It's obvious that Crichton's true calling was screenplay writing, and so far all of the books that I've read by him were made into movies. He often wrote about interesting and very much possible technologies, but I so wish his execution was better. Nevertheless, this novel is fun, easy on the brain, and drowning in enough cheese to make this a guilty pleasure. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('b2Sn2D4TrU7cehyDOt/fHZUTgS4m4/4wYTQHJ+Xr3tS1s4oYY/nUqbsckBWQWb/CvJ36PV2b/p9rhvVVqcPHVA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bkTrOOeUzdFbPkNNAsuV0B0+MVViBRpaa8YTaCfisA+0k8b4un60NjxYz9uoTfUPNLBKRhl9GvVhdOEaa8qpjw==')})
  }

          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1359042651p3/5194.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton">Michael Crichton
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,860
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2918553544661182648">Michael Crichton (1942-2008) was one of the most successful novelists of his generation, admired for his meticulous scientific research and fast-paced narrative. He graduated summa cum laude and earned his MD from Harvard Medical School in 1969. His first novel, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18078737.Odds_On' title='Odds On' rel='nofollow noopener'>Odds On</a>
</i> (1966), was written under the pseudonym <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12619.John_Lange' title='John Lange' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Lange</a> and was followed by seven more Lange novels. He also wrote as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85503.Michael_Douglas' title='Michael Douglas' rel='nofollow noopener'>Michael Doug</a></span>
  <span id="freeText2918553544661182648" style="display:none">Michael Crichton (1942-2008) was one of the most successful novelists of his generation, admired for his meticulous scientific research and fast-paced narrative. He graduated summa cum laude and earned his MD from Harvard Medical School in 1969. His first novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18078737.Odds_On" title="Odds On" rel="nofollow noopener">Odds On</a>
</i> (1966), was written under the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12619.John_Lange" title="John Lange" rel="nofollow noopener">John Lange</a> and was followed by seven more Lange novels. He also wrote as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85503.Michael_Douglas" title="Michael Douglas" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Douglas</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/607284.Jeffery_Hudson" title="Jeffery Hudson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jeffery Hudson</a>. His novel <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7663.A_Case_of_Need" title="A Case of Need" rel="nofollow noopener">A Case of Need</a>
</i> won the Edgar Award in 1969. Popular throughout the world, he has sold more than 200 million books. His novels have been translated into thirty-eight languages, and thirteen have been made into films.<br /><br />Michael Crichton passed away from lymphoma in 2008. He was 66 years old.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2918553544661182648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
